  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_d3fda261-1e28-448b-9a42-84ac52302f51'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

JB promises that if they can fix history, they can all return to their present-day lives. Now Chip and Alex have to reclaim their true identities—as the king and prince of England. But things get complicated when the four discover that according to the records, the princes were murdered. How can they fix history if it means that Chip and Alex will die? Margaret Peterson Haddix is the author of Found, the bestselling Shadow Children series, Uprising, Running Out of Time, and many more</span>

  <span id="freeText9736875675227633076" style="display:none">Have you ever read a "Haddix" book? No, then check out this review. This is my first "Haddix" book and it was incredible. <div id="review_101661814" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101661814" />
    <a title="Loralee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3169163-loralee"><img alt="Loralee" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/101661814?book_show_action=true">May 06, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Loralee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Loralee" href="/user/show/3169163-loralee">Loralee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer101661814" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7661570309800408647">Find a bunch of historical children with tragic destinies. Collect a planeful and drop them in the 20th century. Now, why, exactly, is it so important to put them back? <br/><br/>I never understood.<br/><br/>But Haddix thinks it's important--at least, replacing the missing children is the premise of her book Sent. The collected princes in Richard III's tower are sent back to the moment of their deaths, with only their friends to save them. But what are the friends saving them from? What are they supposed to do, or </span>
  <span id="freeText7661570309800408647" style="display:none">Find a bunch of historical children with tragic destinies. Collect a planeful and drop them in the 20th century. Now, why, exactly, is it so important to put them back? <br /><br />I never understood.<br /><br />But Haddix thinks it's important--at least, replacing the missing children is the premise of her book Sent. The collected princes in Richard III's tower are sent back to the moment of their deaths, with only their friends to save them. But what are the friends saving them from? What are they supposed to do, or not do? And why? <br /><br />Unconvinced by the premise, and confused by the twists and turns (now their technology works, now it doesn't; now they're supposed come out of the past and now they're not), I never really got into the book. Time travel can be tricky even when it's done right, and in this book, the rules seemed to keep changing. No, I didn't care very much that all of time might have been at risk. It didn't feel very risky.<br /><br />The book does end nicely, with a surprise change of heart by Richard the Third, and the epilogue was well done. So you might want to skip straight to the battle at the end. Or not read the book.<br /><br />Middle grade boys might enjoy this series. Fine for young women, though they might get bored. <br /><br />For a better time travel book, read the Doomsday Book by Connie Willis. For a better Haddix book, try the first book in the series, Found, if you don't mind being disappointed by the sequel. In fact, most of her books are brilliant (except Fishers.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="7661570309800408647" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_156591060" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/156591060" />
    <a title="Sesana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5172893-sesana"><img alt="Sesana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1413088169p2/5172893.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/156591060?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sesana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sesana" href="/user/show/5172893-sesana">Sesana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781442407671" href="/book/show/9508482-sent">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172893-sesana?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172893-sesana?shelf=middle-grade">middle-grade</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172893-sesana?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer156591060" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15265764962971024701">Picking up right where the previous book left off, Sent kicks off the actual time traveling portion of the series by stranding viewpoint character Jonah and his friend, Chip, in the 15th century. See, Jonah and Chip (and about thirty other kids) aren't originally from the 21st century. Each of them was born in another time, and whisked away as "endangered" children to be adopted in the future. And now, they're all supposed to be sent back to their original times, to meet their original fates. <br/><br/>Th</span>
  <span id="freeText15265764962971024701" style="display:none">Picking up right where the previous book left off, Sent kicks off the actual time traveling portion of the series by stranding viewpoint character Jonah and his friend, Chip, in the 15th century. See, Jonah and Chip (and about thirty other kids) aren't originally from the 21st century. Each of them was born in another time, and whisked away as "endangered" children to be adopted in the future. And now, they're all supposed to be sent back to their original times, to meet their original fates. <br /><br />This is not the most favorable plan for Chip, or for fellow time-displaced adoptee Alex: they're the missing princes who may or may not have been killed by Richard III, and they've been returned to the night of their disappearance. Naturally, Jonah wants to stop this from happening, and get his friends back to the time they grew up in.<br /><br />I read the first book in this series some time ago, but I remembered enough to keep up. It helped that this particular book was very, very slow. There's a lot of talking about what's going on around them, in great detail. Not just about the historical subjects, but also about the mechanics and principles of the form of time travel used here. In some respects, that's fine. Haddix has obviously put a lot of thought into her history and science fiction. I liked her idea of "timesickness": every molecule in your body has existed in some form from the dawn of time, so when you travel backwards or forwards in time (say, 500 years into the past) your molecules are actually meant to be somewhere else. The side effect is that it makes you feel terrible. I can't recall ever seeing this idea before, and Haddix sold it. I also liked the idea that the time travelers (and only the time travelers) can see echoes (called tracers) of what would have happened in the original version of the timeline. <br /><br />But this is where me not being the target audience for the book comes in. I go into this series with a pretty decent background knowledge of history and how time travel generally works in SF. So the long, detailed explanations of what's going on, both in a historical and science fictional sense, are tedious for me. If I had read this at twelve or thirteen, when I didn't already know exactly what was going to happen to Chip and Alex's original selves, and if I didn't need somebody to patiently explain paradoxes to me, I think I would have enjoyed this book a lot more. Even welcomed the long explanations. <br /><br />I also feel like the general premise, that the children need to be returned to their original timelines, is never adequately explained. <i>Why</i> do they need to be returned? What's purpose will be served, versus everything staying at the new status quo? Especially since meddling with the timelines again couldn't possibly return time to its "original" state. Then again, this is a long series, and the first trip is a bit unplanned, so maybe an explanation will come later on in the series. I just don't think I'll be sticking around to read it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15265764962971024701" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/142486474?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Isaac Morris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Isaac Morris" href="/user/show/4849450-isaac-morris">Isaac Morris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4849450-isaac-morris?shelf=independent-study">independent-study</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer142486474" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4558762711630325619"> Jonah, Chip, Katherine, and a boy named Alex land in the 15th century and find out that Alex is a British prince and Chip is a king. Then they find Chip and Alex’s tracers. (Projections of what time would be like if time travelers hadn’t interfered.) Suddenly, two men come into the room and try to kill Chip and Alex by throwing them out a window. Right before they do, Jonah pulls Chip and Alex away from their tracers. They find out that Richard III, Duke of Gloucester, is trying to kill Chip an</span>
  <span id="freeText4558762711630325619" style="display:none"> Jonah, Chip, Katherine, and a boy named Alex land in the 15th century and find out that Alex is a British prince and Chip is a king. Then they find Chip and Alex’s tracers. (Projections of what time would be like if time travelers hadn’t interfered.) Suddenly, two men come into the room and try to kill Chip and Alex by throwing them out a window. Right before they do, Jonah pulls Chip and Alex away from their tracers. They find out that Richard III, Duke of Gloucester, is trying to kill Chip and Alex so he can be king. They go hide with Chip and Alex’s mother, the former queen of England. While they are there, JB (one of their friends) pulls Jonah and Katherine out of time. He tells them that Chip and Alex need to stay for two more years. Then he drops them back into time two years later. They meet back up with Chip and Alex and JB tells them that they can pull Chip and Alex out of time after Richard III sees them in the battle. When the time comes, they have a hard time separated Chip from his tracer. They finally get him and tell JB that they’re ready. Then one of the soldiers pulls out an Elucinator (a future gun/computer) and they go to JB’s hideout. They argue with JB so that they can go back to their normal life. They have a normal life for about two months then JB tells them they have to help put another girl back into time.<br /> The author uses many descriptive words that paint a really good picture in the readers mind. All of her books are action and mystery books. I like all the suspense in her plots.<br /> Literary Terms:<br />Protagonist- Jonah, Katherine, Chip, and Alex<br />Antagonist-Richard III<br />Setting- In the 15th century in England<br />Point of view- third person-says “Jonah” instead of “I”, says “I” in Jonahs thoughts. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4558762711630325619" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText10292583248559928425" style="display:none">The first book of the series was definitely better than this one, and less confusing. I read a few time travel books before, (all with altering the future precautions...and stuff) but this one probably provided more confusion than most.<br /><br />I liked that it was based on a real historical mystery, but I'm not big on English rule and all, so I wasn't <i>that</i> sure if it was real or not until I looked it up. Tracers were something new, and I liked them. The idea was really neat; I'd rather have them than not, but without them, I think, the book would've had extreme differences, like the ending, for example.<br /><br />The characters were all right: realistic(somewhat), likable, distinct. I like Chip/Edward's rule/power hungry attitude - I think royalty is like that, not all the of them, of course, but a few. One thing's surprising though: in most books (relating to romance, or is it just the YA-romance books?) you usually see only romance between the main character and someone else, or at least their romance is the first that is evident, so I enjoyed the change.<br /><br />The book also sets up for the third book in the series...I wonder if Haddix is going to do a book for all the stolen/missing children. </span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3037969886" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3037969886" />
    <a title="Joshua Barnett" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59397795-joshua-barnett"><img alt="Joshua Barnett" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473260726p2/59397795.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3037969886?book_show_action=true">Dec 12, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joshua Barnett" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joshua Barnett" href="/user/show/59397795-joshua-barnett">Joshua Barnett</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer3037969886&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3037969886" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4596667442628930491"><i>The Missing: Sent</i><br/><b>Personal Response</b><br/>I think the book, <i>The Missing: Sent</i> by Margaret Peterson Haddix, like the first one, is very nice and pretty easy to read. I feel as though I would enjoy the book even more if it is a little bit more of a challenge to read. The plot is very nicely paced out, and the characters all got time to grow and develop. I am looking forward to the next book, <i>The Missing: Sabotaged</i>, in the series.<br/><br/><b>Plot</b><br/>The beginning of this book starts off where the last one ends with the ki</span>
  <span id="freeText4596667442628930491" style="display:none"><i>The Missing: Sent</i><br /><b>Personal Response</b><br />I think the book, <i>The Missing: Sent</i> by Margaret Peterson Haddix, like the first one, is very nice and pretty easy to read. I feel as though I would enjoy the book even more if it is a little bit more of a challenge to read. The plot is very nicely paced out, and the characters all got time to grow and develop. I am looking forward to the next book, <i>The Missing: Sabotaged</i>, in the series.<br /><br /><b>Plot</b><br />The beginning of this book starts off where the last one ends with the kids in the 15th century. They are starting to discover how they affect the past and how tracers work. While trying to remain hidden, the children hear footsteps coming towards the room they are in. Jonah tries and fails to figure out how to use the elucidator. Chip decides to kick it and consequently breaks it. They run over to where it lands and it reads, “beginning repairs click to view options.” They learn how to use the invisibility option, allowing them to become unseen by anyone. Two men come in whom try to kill Alex and Chip. Jonah, Katherine, Chip, and Alex are all able to hide from the killers until morning. After they find a little bit of food, they all travel to a cathedral to stop Chip and Alex’s uncle from becoming King. This does not go as planned and they become visible again. While running away, they run into the two boys’ mother. Chip and Alex start talking to their “mother” JB pulls Katherine and Jonah out of time to talk to them about what is going on. The two siblings must leave their new friends in the 15th century for two years before they can be saved. Katherine and Jonah travel two years into the future to save the boys. <br /><br /><b>Characterization</b><br />In this book, Jonah continues to grow into the leader he never thought he would be. Katherine starts moving away from the prissy, young cheerleader she yearns to be into a bright, young woman who will soon become a great leader.<br /><br />Another character who grows a lot in this story is Chip and Alex’s Uncle. At the beginning of the story, he tries to have his nephews killed. Their uncle, Richard III, is seen as an evil man without a heart who is only interested in being in power. With a little nudging from the invisible Katherine and Jonah, he begins to feel remorse for what he did. Near the end of the story, the reader begins to almost feel bad for him because he is seen crying and repenting to God about what he has done.<br /><br /><b>Setting</b> <br />This book takes place mostly in 15th-century England. This is important because of how Jonah and Katherine are trying to save Chip and Alex from being killed. Occasionally, Jonah and Katherine travel with JB to a “Time Hole”: a place where time does not exist. This is an important part of the plot because it allows them to communicate with no outside distractions.<br /><br /><b>Thematic Connection</b><br />The theme of fulfillment of one’s word is very prevalent in the book <i>The Missing: Sent</i>. All of the characters make promises at one point or another and always keep them. This is shown by Jonah when he and Katherine go into the past to save Chip and Alex from being murdered because they promised to save them and make time right again. <br />Another theme that this book touches on is the theme of loyalty. No matter what hardship any of the characters go through, they always stay loyal to each other. They also remain loyal to their original goal to keep all of the missing children safe.<br /><br /><b>Recommendation</b><br />I would recommend this book to any middle school readers and up. While this book is an easier read vocabulary wise, its meaning is a little deeper at times. This book could be enjoyed by any gender since there are strong characters of both sexes.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4596667442628930491" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1933611906" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1933611906" />
    <a title="Gus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23890975-gus"><img alt="Gus" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1933611906?book_show_action=true">Mar 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gus" href="/user/show/23890975-gus">Gus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer1933611906&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1933611906" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1349967934904521509">Personal Response<br/>I thought that this book was a great addition to the series. The plot in this book was full of details and had a lot of action. The characters in the book were very interesting to read about. The science fiction concepts in the book kept me thinking throughout the book. The history details in the book were also very cool to read about. <br/><br/>Plot<br/>Jonah, Chip, Alex, and Katherine were all sent back into time by JB. The group of kids had no clue where and when in time they were. The grou</span>
  <span id="freeText1349967934904521509" style="display:none">Personal Response<br />I thought that this book was a great addition to the series. The plot in this book was full of details and had a lot of action. The characters in the book were very interesting to read about. The science fiction concepts in the book kept me thinking throughout the book. The history details in the book were also very cool to read about. <br /><br />Plot<br />Jonah, Chip, Alex, and Katherine were all sent back into time by JB. The group of kids had no clue where and when in time they were. The group found out that they were sent back to London in the fifteenth century. Chip and Alex realized that they were the missing princes of England and that they had arrived in their rightful time. The kids traveled throughout London looking for ways to correct that past and go home. They figured out that Chip and Alex were originally assassinated before they were stolen from their original time. The kids fake the assassination and spend their time in the past looking for ways to get back to the twenty-first century. The kids were able to contact JB, only to find out that their work was not done. Jonah and Katherine had to transport two years later and leave Chip and Alex behind. Jonah and Katherine arrived two years later and helped their friends through the Battle of Bosworth. JB beamed the kids back to the twenty-first century and told them that he may need their help again.<br /><br />Characterization<br />The arrival into their original time period took a toll on Chip and Alex. When the two had to join with their tracers, it started to affect their minds. They started to remember who they truly were and their memories warped their personalities. They became more self entitled and started to disregard their future selves. Chip and Alex couldn't remember who their friends and family from the future were. <br /><br />Impact of Setting/Time<br />The setting in the book was fifteenth century England. The setting of the book was very impactful on the main characters. The fifteenth century was more dangerous than any place the characters had ever been. The people in this time period were very scared of the supernatural so time travel could have killed the main characters. They had to be careful to disrupt the balance of time and keep away from the people around them. The time period in the book was the fifteenth century. The lack of technology in the fifteenth century also played a major role in the book. When the characters faced a problem, they had to find creative ways to solve it.<br /><br />Thematic Connection<br />I think the theme throughout the book involved helping the people in their life. Jonah and Katherine went back in time to make sure that they wouldn't lose their friend Chip. They did whatever they could while in the past to make sure that Chip could come back to the future with them. Jonah and Katherine risked their lives all across London to help solve Chip's past. <br /><br />Rating/Recommendation<br />This book was very deserving of all five stars. The plot was very exciting and it was a huge reason why I gave this book five stars. The historical information and time travel in the book were also reasons why I gave this book all five stars. I would recommend this book to anyone who enjoyed the first book of the series. I would also recommend it to anyone who enjoys science fiction or books that involve a little bit of history. This book was great for anyone middle school and up due to its overall lack of complexity. Since the book has both male and female characters, I would recommend this book to both male and female readers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1349967934904521509" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2950938021?book_show_action=true">Aug 29, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian" href="/user/show/2844465-brian">Brian</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2950938021" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8195608414035331404">We had this book hanging around from the library, so even though I was not impressed by the first one, I thought I would give the next book a try. My main frustration with book one was that it was all a build up to an idea that we didn't really get any payoff for, so I figured that this book would give that payoff. It did, but it was not any better as a book. <br/>The same irritations were present for me--the kids are obnoxious, they spout out vocabulary words, concepts, and historical trivia all the</span>
  <span id="freeText8195608414035331404" style="display:none">We had this book hanging around from the library, so even though I was not impressed by the first one, I thought I would give the next book a try. My main frustration with book one was that it was all a build up to an idea that we didn't really get any payoff for, so I figured that this book would give that payoff. It did, but it was not any better as a book. <br />The same irritations were present for me--the kids are obnoxious, they spout out vocabulary words, concepts, and historical trivia all the time and then they have to give annoying and unbelievable "reasons" for knowing those things. I have a hard time believing that sixth grade social studies covers the kinds of things that the author claims it did (like dictators killing off political prisoners and saying "they died trying to escape"... that's just one of many, many dumb examples). And that's just one small piece of why this book annoyed and disappointed me. <br />But I won't go on ragging on a book intended for children. I just won't be reading any further in the series, and I don't think I'll be encouraging my children to continue with it either. There are much better things for them to read than this.</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3070221078" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer3949550197003180848">I have actually read this book many times before. I love the excitement and the action mixed with all of the Sci-Fi type plots in the book. I really enjoyed the drama in this book too. There are all of the problems with JB that cause Katherine, Chip, Jonah, and Alex to question whether or not he really is there to help them.   <span id="freeText3949550197003180848" style="display:none">I have actually read this book many times before. I love the excitement and the action mixed with all of the Sci-Fi type plots in the book. I really enjoyed the drama in this book too. There are all of the problems with JB that cause Katherine, Chip, Jonah, and Alex to question whether or not he really is there to help them. Then with the crush spreading between Katherine and Chip just makes the book more enjoyable than it already is. When she explains to Chip why she never says yes to other boys and then kisses him, it pulls Chip out of his tracer and creates a whole new excitement level for the book. This book has lots of details that help better explain what happens in the story. The details create a whole new perspective for me as to what happens and why in the book. Even the little things in the story like the color of the rock or the design on a tapestry all have a certain meaning in the book. All of these factors create a wonderful second book to the series. I love this book and I really enjoyed this mix of Sci-Fi, Realistic Fiction, and action. <br /></span>

<div id="review_231418488" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/231418488" />
    <a title="Michelle Isenhoff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5319195-michelle-isenhoff"><img alt="Michelle Isenhoff" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1556827290p2/5319195.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/231418488?book_show_action=true">Nov 05, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michelle Isenhoff" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michelle Isenhoff" href="/user/show/5319195-michelle-isenhoff">Michelle Isenhoff</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer231418488" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10787189647564256124">I don’t always review more than one book in a series, but in this case, the sequel was as good as the original. At the end of book one, Jonah, Katherine and Chip find themselves whirling through time. In book two, they emerge in the year 1483. Because Chip isn’t just their 21st century neighbor, he’s Edward V, King of England, and he’s about to become worm food. To prevent “the ripple” from affecting all of time, it’s crucial that Chip return to the years he was stolen from so events will play o</span>
  <span id="freeText10787189647564256124" style="display:none">I don’t always review more than one book in a series, but in this case, the sequel was as good as the original. At the end of book one, Jonah, Katherine and Chip find themselves whirling through time. In book two, they emerge in the year 1483. Because Chip isn’t just their 21st century neighbor, he’s Edward V, King of England, and he’s about to become worm food. To prevent “the ripple” from affecting all of time, it’s crucial that Chip return to the years he was stolen from so events will play out according to history. But how can they let that happen and still rescue Chip?<br /> <br />This book got into even more mind-boggling time travel elements than the first one. When they land, the trio use their Elucidator to turn themselves invisible, so their hair and clothing don’t draw unwanted unwanted attention. They struggle with the Old English language, though Haddix does a good job creating this problem without actually using too many of the kid-baffling words. Time travel sickness becomes a problem, too, for Jonah and Katherine, who are out of their original century. After all, the atoms which make up their bodies are actually in use elsewhere during this time period, so they’re made of duplicate substances. And the kids must also watch out for other time travelers. For instance, the Battle of Boswell was such a mysterious event that it has attracted many witnesses from future ages. Bizarre!<br /> <br />Here in the Past, the kids also run into tracers, glowing, phantom-like images visible only to the kids, that show them exactly what happened in history. When Chip merges with his tracer, he becomes visible to those in that world as Edward, and he gains that boy’s memories, religious beliefs and thoughts while still able to maintain his own memories. He can leave his tracer anytime he wants, but the longer he stays in it, the more he forgets the 21st century. Tracers, both Chip’s and others, glow anytime events deviate from history, so the kids can gauge the effects of their actions.<br /> <br />Again, Haddix keeps her book very clean, with no profanity. She’s dealing with an unsavory royal family at a brutal time in history, so the children do watch the tracers of Edward and his brother being tossed out the window in an attempted murder. The book also very appropriately discusses the “married” or “not married” issue of Edward’s parents and how it affects his claim to the throne. And it touches on the religion vs. science difference between the ages without flatly slamming religion.<br /> <br />I also really like the humorous way Chip is affected by his time travel experiences, how he takes away a knowledge of Latin and a talent for sword fighting. “You get what you get,” an experienced time traveler tells him. “Time changes you. Time traveler or no, you always have to build on what you live through.” Good advice for the rest of us too.<br /> <br />In conclusion, I highly recommend Sent. It’s a fabulous, entertaining, thought-provoking adventure, one of the best I’ve read this year, and entirely appropriate for readers 8-13.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10787189647564256124" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3155065874" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3155065874" />
    <a title="Mason Alsteen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/88073179-mason-alsteen"><img alt="Mason Alsteen" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3155065874?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mason Alsteen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mason Alsteen" href="/user/show/88073179-mason-alsteen">Mason Alsteen</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3155065874" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13337516139525092356">Mason Alsteen<br/>Book title: <i>The Missing: Sent</i><br/>Lexile:730L<br/><br/>Personal Response: I did not exactly like the book because it has to do with a lot of teen angst. The main character Jonah is kind of whiney through the whole book as well as scared. Katherine is kind of bossy as well as being an outcast. Chip is really angry about the whole of him having to play dead. Alex is really quiet which was cool but also got very boring.<br/><br/>Plot: It’s dark. Jonah, Katherine, Chip, and Alex had just traveled back in time t</span>
  <span id="freeText13337516139525092356" style="display:none">Mason Alsteen<br />Book title: <i>The Missing: Sent</i><br />Lexile:730L<br /><br />Personal Response: I did not exactly like the book because it has to do with a lot of teen angst. The main character Jonah is kind of whiney through the whole book as well as scared. Katherine is kind of bossy as well as being an outcast. Chip is really angry about the whole of him having to play dead. Alex is really quiet which was cool but also got very boring.<br /><br />Plot: It’s dark. Jonah, Katherine, Chip, and Alex had just traveled back in time to the year 1483. As part of a deal with future time travelers, Jonah promised to try and save Chip and Alex from dying in 1483. After they had gotten used to their surroundings, they opened the door to another room where they saw two ghostly lights. The Elucidator, which Jonah stole, started talking. JB, the time-traveling good guy, explained that what they were seeing were the tracers or the real timeline versions of what events were supposed to happen and that every time they see a tracer it means that some event was changed in the past. Jonah thought it a good idea that they try not to mess with tracers, much to no avail, Chip and Alex walked over to the tracers and started to mess around, Chip has just dared Alex to try and mimic his tracer and was merged into it. The rest of the kids saw what appeared to be a twenty-first boy in medieval-looking clothes.<br />	Chip, thinking it was cool, decided to merge with his tracer, just as Chip merged with the ghostly blue light, Jonah and Katherine heard footsteps coming from the hallway. Jonah thought that twenty-first-century kids being there would alter history so he decided to blow out the candle that lit the room, just as two men came bursting into the room, Jonah and Katherine ducked for cover. Being invisible, Jonah hid behind a tapestry while Katherine, Alex, and Chip could not find a place to hide. As the men were walking around the room with a poorly light candle, they kept almost running into the three kids. Chip and Alex who were jumping around trying to avoid the men accidentally merged with their tracers and suddenly appeared. Being in a dark room the men did not see the boys emerge out of thin air, but did catch them as they made another sweep. The men dragged the two boys over to the window and proceeded to toss them out of the tower. Jonah’s reflexes kicked in and he rushed over to the window and pulled up Chip and Alex. History once again being altered because Jonah had pulled the boys up and to safety, they separated from their tracers and saw as Katherine was still avoiding the men when an ember had landed on her hair. She quickly put out the fire and saw as the men were in complete and utter shock as a fire had just spawned out of thin air. Being completely freaked out and thought that it was witchcraft, the two men had run away leaving the kids completely alone in the dark. The kids just dozed off hoping that their invisibility would not go away overnight and that they could just rest in peace.<br />	The group woke up at relatively the same time and realized that they are still invisible. A servant girl walked in and wondered where the princes were, stating “They’s always abed when I come in.” she thought that the princes were just in the bathroom, leaving the food tray at the foot of the bed and then exiting the room. The teens, realizing that they are starving, walk over to the food tray. One hour later, the teens finally leave the tower of London and take a trip down the river Thames to Westminster Abbey for the coronation ceremony of King Richard III., when they arrive there is already a huge crowd all cheering for the new king. Chip despised that the people where agreeing with everything King Richard was doing and charged the soon-to-be king, luckily Katherine caught him and dragged him to the church. Fuming about not getting his chance to kill the king and restore himself as leader, Jonah quickly thought of a plan to scare Richard into letting go of his crown, in a private room where Richard was all alone the teens enacted their plan and with the help of their invisibility, play the part of angels communing with a God fearing man. The teens accuse the new found king of killing his nephews to the point in which he tells them that he did it for the good of the kingdom. Just as the teens were going to make Richard give up the crown, the Elucidator that was broken repaired itself and reset. Exposing the teens and making Richard angry. The teens escape through a tunnel and run into a group of monks where Katherine is mocked and furious. Deciding to go to a sister church the group venture to meet up with Chips mom and sisters. They distract the guards by slipping past them with the Elucidator’s invisibility mode. Inside they are surprised to see the tracers of the two boys sitting in the chairs. Looking over and seeing their mother and sisters crying Chip and Alex decide to remerge with their tracers. Leaving Jonah and Katherine they appeared in front of their mother and rejoiced as Jonah and Katherine are pulled out of time.<br />	Sitting in a white room, Jonah and Katherine are waiting for JB to appear and say something. JB does appear out of thin air and gives the children praise but also scolds them. A large screen appears in front of the three and shows the exact moment that JB pulled Jonah and Katherine out of time. JB says that in order for the timeline to remain intact that he must fast forward two years, much to Jonah and Katherine’s protest.<br />	Sending them two years into the past’s future they arrive outside King Richard’s tent outside of Bosworth. Sneaking inside they wake the king, as he is on his knees Katherine says that the only way to forgiveness was to renounce the crown and to live a better life. The two then leave the tent and end up on a hill. The battle begins and they find Chip and Alex but so does King Richard. The king relinquishes his title and charges alone in battle. Chip and Alex also charge into battle but are pulled out at the last second as a warrior was about to kill them with an ax. They arrive in the present just like nothing happened.<br /><br />Characterization: Jonah was really worried about the timeline throughout the entire book. Jonah never stopped worrying but it began to hide better and was eventually replaced with courage and intelligence.<br />	Katherine didn’t really change much but towards the beginning of the book, she was mocked as a woman by a group of monks that the four ran into while escaping, becoming furious by how women were treated in the fifteenth century.<br />	Chip had the most development throughout the entire book as he was basically the main focus of the book. When Chip first learned that he was the rightful King of England he was ecstatic, but once he learned that his throne was stolen by his uncle it all changed. When they got to England the group was separated by time travelers, Two years had passed and Chip had grown up now. At the Battle of Bosworth, Chip’s uncle Richard decided to return the crown back to Chip. If Chip and Alex were not separated from their tracers they would have fallen in battle. Chip forgives Richard and is returned to the future.<br /><br />Setting: The setting takes place in 1483 to 1485. The seasons are fall and summer respectively, in the Tower of London, Buckingham Palace, the Battle of Bosworth, and in Ohio in the present day. Also out of time in a time dimension. These places are important because it involves the rule of King Richard III who was a very unpleasant king.<br />Recommendation: I recommend this to any teenager, both male and female, that likes time travel and loves personal struggle. This is a good book for kids who have been adopted and find an outlet in the characters as they wish that they too could be the son or daughter of someone important.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13337516139525092356" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_77577545" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/77577545" />
    <a title="Eva Mitnick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/633929-eva-mitnick"><img alt="Eva Mitnick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1237933117p2/633929.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/77577545?book_show_action=true">Nov 12, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eva Mitnick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eva Mitnick" href="/user/show/633929-eva-mitnick">Eva Mitnick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/633929-eva-mitnick?shelf=children">children</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/633929-eva-mitnick?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer77577545" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1611986294153507914">A fast-moving, conspiracy-filled plot made Book 1 of this series - Found - a page-turner. Unfortunately, 99% of Sent takes place in the 1480s where the action moves rather more slowly. Yes, there is a bit of political intrigue, the danger of being discovered, and of course the worry that one will affect time adversely. However, it never feels like the kids are in any real danger and thus this reads like a slightly more intense installment of the Magic Tree House series. This is probably due to t</span>
  <span id="freeText1611986294153507914" style="display:none">A fast-moving, conspiracy-filled plot made Book 1 of this series - Found - a page-turner. Unfortunately, 99% of Sent takes place in the 1480s where the action moves rather more slowly. Yes, there is a bit of political intrigue, the danger of being discovered, and of course the worry that one will affect time adversely. However, it never feels like the kids are in any real danger and thus this reads like a slightly more intense installment of the Magic Tree House series. This is probably due to the fact that the time-traveling adult who is helping, JB, can not only communicate with them but can actually pull them "out of time" when he needs to give them facts or equipment. Speaking of equipment, they have a handy-dandy device called an Elucidator that not only translates the Middle English speech all around them, but can make them invisible. This major cop-out took away a lot of the danger and suspense.<br /><br />That said, the writing is confident and fluid. Jonah, whose point of view we share, is a practical and likable boy and, while he doesn't exactly glow with charisma and personality, he is certainly believable. Less believable is his cheerleader sister who, though not yet 12 years old, is not only fearless but uses phrases like "perpetuate a stereotype." Chip comes across as a boy with - no surprise - a chip on his shoulder, and Alex is the know-it-all Maynard character. More interesting than the slightly bland characters is a feature of time-travel called "tracers." When a time-traveler causes a person to act differently than he would have without the interference, the tracer is like a ghost, depicting the person still on the course history should have gone - so if I went back in time and bumped into a person on the street, he might stop momentarily while I apologized, but meanwhile his tracer would be continuing down the street as if nothing had happened. It's a fascinating idea, although one that raises more questions the more one thinks about it, which seems to the norm with time-travel concepts (look at all the discussion raised by When You Reach Me by Rebecca Stead).<br /><br />So - compared with the thrilling premise of the first book, this installment was merely a typical travel-back-in-time adventure. It will continue in book 3 with Jonah and Katherine being sent back in time to work out the time kinks caused by the snatching of Virginia Dare back in colonial days. Will some original elements be introduced, or will this series devolve into The Magic Tree House for slightly older kids? Only time will tell.<br /><br />Recommended for those who read and enjoyed Book 1 - ages 10 - 12.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1611986294153507914" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4035409522501819778">Personal response<br/>I thought this book was was easy to enjoy, but I thought the author could have added more detail. I found myself getting lost with what was going on because the details were not very good. The author added a little bit of detail here and there, but not enough to make the story easy to understand. <br/><br/>Plot<br/>Sent is book number two in The Child of the Missing series. It is about Jonah and his younger sister, Katherine, going back to the 16th century and returning Jonah's best friend Chi</span>
  <span id="freeText4035409522501819778" style="display:none">Personal response<br />I thought this book was was easy to enjoy, but I thought the author could have added more detail. I found myself getting lost with what was going on because the details were not very good. The author added a little bit of detail here and there, but not enough to make the story easy to understand. <br /><br />Plot<br />Sent is book number two in The Child of the Missing series. It is about Jonah and his younger sister, Katherine, going back to the 16th century and returning Jonah's best friend Chip (who was the prince of England) and his “brother” Alex (the Duke of York). The problem is that Chip and Alex’s uncle, King Richard III is planning to murder the two of them for the thrown. The only way to prevent this is to spark a war against there own kingdom and overthrow the corrupt king. <br /><br />Characterization<br />The major character in this book is 12 year old Jonah Skidmore. Jonah is adopted by the Skidmores on Christmas Eve when he was less than a year old. He lived 12 years in Listion, US, but in those years he feels like alien. In the beginning, Jonah is selfish and only cares for himself. In his new, and unexpected adventures, he has taken more care of his sister than himself. <br /><br />Impacts of Setting<br />This book takes place in the early 15 century in the time of Middle-Age England. There is no specific date given, just the small unknown town. The reason the four of them are in this is to return chip and alex to there proper time. There is a plague going around, but Chip, Alex, Jonah and Katherine are immune due to 21st century medicine. Towards the end of the story the setting changes to an open field battle between two nations. <br /><br />Thematic Connection <br />The theme that fits this book is the theme of choices and possibilities. I say this because Jonah is making choices and weighing out the possibility to where he belongs. Jonah, also makes the right choice to change time for the good Some of the ways that he makes the choice for good is that he prevents the “past” Chip and Alex suicide. Another way that he changes time for the good is attempt to make the king act mad and crazy so the kingdom will go against his rule.<br />Audience<br />I would recommend the book, Sent, to boys around middle school to high school ages. Boys tend to like science fiction more so than girls and this book is just that. The time travel and science fiction can confuse someone at time, which is why I would recommend this book to middle school and high school age kids. <br /><br />Rating<br />I rate this book 5 stars because it is a great book even though it can get a little confusing with all the time travel going on. The plot is also confusing, but sometimes I am up for the challenge. <br /><br /><br /><br /></span>

  <span id="freeText5278504613123584572" style="display:none">1 book<br />315 pages<br /><br /> This book is about three kids that get sent back in time. this is the second book in the missing book series. In book 1 an unexpected airplane that landed in the airport and was filled up with babies and they are all sent out to orphanages around the country. later on they all find out that they were on that plane and they are missing kids. this means there were kidnapped out of there real time period and sent to the current time period because they were famous kings that had something extremely bad happen to them. they are sent back by a man named J.B (NOT JUSTIN BEIBER!!!!) and are there to make the time period the way that it is supposed to be. when they get there they are invisible ghost but later on in the book when they are getting closer with there real roots they turn into real people and they find out how to make it right. There friend Alex was a king of England that was assassinated. This meant that to make the time period right they would have to let Alex be assassinated. They couldn't do that so they made up a plan to fake Alex's death so everyone thinks he was killed but he was not and they can return home with there friend. then there communication device is broken so they cant communicate home to tell them when to take them back so they were stuck in the 1400's. when they get away with faking there death they leave to go back to the current time and then Chip and Jonah are waiting and then Kathrine and Alex went into battle so they were abandoned in the 1400 century.<br /><br /> This book is a very good book. this book is a good book because there is a lot of action. I have really enjoyed this book because I read book one and it was very good. I really like this author and have read other series by her and cant wait to get book 3! </span>

<div id="review_1284748459" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1284748459" />
    <a title="Robert Karbowiak" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34820697-robert-karbowiak"><img alt="Robert Karbowiak" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1284748459?book_show_action=true">May 19, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robert Karbowiak" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robert Karbowiak" href="/user/show/34820697-robert-karbowiak">Robert Karbowiak</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1284748459" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9064422695294562049">Sent, by Margaret Peterson Haddix, is the second book of the Missing Series. The main characters, Jonah and Katherine, along with their friends Chip and Alex, travel back in time to the fifteenth century. There they must prevent Chip’s and Alex’s death from criminals seeking to kill them. Jonah, Chip, and Alex find out that they are all children taken from history and sold into adoption to live in the 21st century, rather than their original time periods. Chip and Alex, in history, are royalty. </span>
  <span id="freeText9064422695294562049" style="display:none">Sent, by Margaret Peterson Haddix, is the second book of the Missing Series. The main characters, Jonah and Katherine, along with their friends Chip and Alex, travel back in time to the fifteenth century. There they must prevent Chip’s and Alex’s death from criminals seeking to kill them. Jonah, Chip, and Alex find out that they are all children taken from history and sold into adoption to live in the 21st century, rather than their original time periods. Chip and Alex, in history, are royalty. They are the monarchs of England. Jonah, well, no one really knows what time period Jonah’s really from yet. That is the basis of all the books of the series so far: the whacky adventures Jonah and Katherine go on trying to find out Jonah’s true identity. But instead, they get intertwined in other missing kids’ affairs; they’ve had to travel through a time cave so far, so there’s no telling what will happen in later novels. This is a great book and a must read for anyone who likes a good suspense and thriller novel. This series, as far as two books, has already hooked me on, along with the Gatekeepers, by another author. The Missing Series is an amazing series with rarely found suspense like this. The book keeps a person reading like none other, as do the other multitudes of books by Margaret Peterson Haddix; the rest of the series alone is something, something that once read makes a person wonder how they couldn’t have read that book earlier. It’s like no other series I’ve read in my entire life, and I see myself as a reading enthusiast. On a boring day, I just crack open the next book of this series, or just finish the current one. Whatever it is, it is the most viable cure to boredom I can think of.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9064422695294562049" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>

<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22750413-salsa"><img alt="Salsa: Un poema para cocinar / A Cooking Poem" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1409185200l/22750413._SX98_.jpg" />

<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('coE8OdJdUvHdhI/7/QZhJKvGZhkAYDhY9Caqt5Dy2jn4RLSJE/ZWN5eI1zOlNN3CoKlY4z7auB5R92/zW+/Eag==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/14617.Margaret_Peterson_Haddix">About Margaret Peterson Haddix</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/14617.Margaret_Peterson_Haddix"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1309842407p3/14617.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/14617.Margaret_Peterson_Haddix">Margaret Peterson Haddix
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
5,252
followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17045419851890044006">Margaret Peterson Haddix grew up on a farm near Washington Court House, Ohio. She graduated from Miami University (of Ohio) with degrees in English/journalism, English/creative writing and history. Before her first book was published, she worked as a newspaper copy editor in Fort Wayne, Indiana; a newspaper reporter in Indianapolis; and a community college instructor and freelance writer in Danvil</span>
  <span id="freeText17045419851890044006" style="display:none">Margaret Peterson Haddix grew up on a farm near Washington Court House, Ohio. She graduated from Miami University (of Ohio) with degrees in English/journalism, English/creative writing and history. Before her first book was published, she worked as a newspaper copy editor in Fort Wayne, Indiana; a newspaper reporter in Indianapolis; and a community college instructor and freelance writer in Danville, Illinois. <br /><br /> She has since written more than 25 books for kids and teens, including <i>Running Out of Time</i>; <i>Don’t You Dare Read This, Mrs. Dunphrey</i>; <i>Leaving Fishers</i>; <i>Just Ella</i>; <i>Turnabout</i>; <i>Takeoffs and Landings</i>; <i>The Girl with 500 Middle Names</i>; <i>Because of Anya</i>; <i>Escape from Memory</i>; <i>Say What?</i>; <i>The House on the Gulf</i>; <i>Double Identity</i>; <i>Dexter the Tough</i>; <i>Uprising</i>; <i>Palace of Mirrors</i>; <i>Claim to Fame</i>; the <i>Shadow Children</i> series; and the <i>Missing</i> series. She also wrote <i>Into the Gauntlet</i>, the tenth book in the <i>39 Clues</i> series. Her books have been honored with <i>New York Times</i> bestseller status, the International Reading Association’s Children’s Book Award; American Library Association Best Book and Quick Pick for Reluctant Young Adult Readers notations; and more than a dozen state reader’s choice awards.<br /><br /><br /> Haddix and her husband, Doug, now live in Columbus, Ohio, with their two children.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17045419851890044006" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/3944773-sent">Quotes from Sent</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;But did he go to heaven?" Katherine persisted. <br />"That's between him and God, not him and history," JB said.<br />Alex started, jerking so spastically that he kicked the basketball and would have sent it spinning out into the street if Chip hadn't caught it. Amazingly, Chip still seemed to have a swordsman's quick reflexes.<br />"YOU believe in God?" Alex asked JB incredulously. "But you know how to travel through time. You're a scientist." He hesitated. "Aren't you?"<br />JB rolled his eyes.<br />"It amazes me how people of your time set up such a false dichotomy between science and religion. Fortunately, that only lasts for another... well, I can't tell you that," he said, stopping himself just in time. "But I assure you, the more I travel through time, the more I witness, the more I realize that there are things that are both strange and wonderful, far beyond human comprehension." (pgs 299-300)&rdquo;</span>

Does that mean I'm going to be a famous philosopher in the future? Does that mean I'm going to be revered? There wasn't time to ask.&rdquo;</span>
